Ocean, Mountains, and Desert 192

— Ling Mo

Louis Auchincloss, at the beginning of his Mediterranean cruise journal, said: "Finally, I left behind the life on land, all the bustling affairs, the noisy clamor. Although it has only been a quarter of an hour since boarding the ship, and the ship is just a floating hotel, it is still surprising how the eyes, ears, mouth, and nose could endure life on land for so long before." The ocean certainly possesses great, profound, changeable, and adventurous allure, but for those who go to sea, the number attracted by the ocean's charm is probably no more than those compelled by the magic of the land. Nowadays, the ocean is a thoroughfare for gold-plated people, but we haven't heard of any gold-plated person loving the sea. The sea is not something loved by those seeking a golden kingdom, and it is almost an obstacle. But those who love the sea do not want to reach a golden kingdom through it; instead, they hope for a ghost ship that will "float forever on the sea without relying on a port," allowing them to leave the land forever. If forced to reach land, they might only hope for an unknown country. In the past, our world was vast, and seafarers could often find new lands. Although new lands quickly fell under the influence of the old world, there was still freer breathing in the new lands. Rebellious and oppressed people were constantly willing to go overseas. North America was ultimately created into a country by some rebels. The Chinese in Southeast Asia could have played out a similar story, but perhaps because they did not need constant struggle after arriving in the new land, they gradually restored the so-called peaceful virtues of the Chinese, and bowed their heads under the sudden intrusion of Western powers. After that, only the empty name of the Chinese Sea remained, and in reality, we lost the ocean. Although in recent years those places have still been the destination for some people forced to go to sea, these are only small corners of the world, no longer the destinations hoped for by seafarers. We think we know the whole world, but the world is actually too small, at least made small by a part of humanity living on it. In fact, since there cannot be a ghost ship that never relies on a port, and there is no way to choose a more ideal port, everywhere is the same land, a point that cannot be temporarily forgotten even on a ship. Naturally, the dreams of sea lovers become even emptier dreams. However, by keeping this dream, we may one day restore the lovable ocean.

Besides the sea, mountains are another beautiful creation of nature. In China, it seems that people generally love mountains more than the sea. Mountains are originally solemn, profound, and ruggedly lovely, but what the Chinese love does not seem to be these qualities. We have so-called mountain and forest literature, but it is merely an attempt to find a novel embellishment in elegance, or at most, a way to express personal grievances. These grievances are often not extreme, perhaps because poets should be gentle and kind, but perhaps also because they fear cutting off the shortcut to officialdom in the Zhongnan Mountains. This is probably also the reason why hermits do not want to go to sea but want to enter the mountains. Going to sea may mean never returning, while entering the mountains has a day of leaving them. Thus, entering the mountains is naturally not too deep. You only need to open the biographies of hermits in historical books to prove it. They create many famous mountains for us, and because of repeated imitation, the fame of these mountains grows, but these famous mountains do not have any majestic or grand meaning. Truly deep mountains and great valleys are only inhabited by rebellious bandits. Just like the starving people and pirates who crossed the sea to foreign lands, no maritime literature is left to us; mountain kings will not leave us any mountain and forest literature either. We do not know much about the famous mountains within the country, even those not in remote areas. As for the "Roof of the World," the highest plateau, and the highest peak far on the borders, we have no way of knowing them until they bear foreign names like Austen and Everest. We cannot understand why people travel thousands of miles, climb those desolate areas full of ice and snow and fraught with danger. We do not understand pioneering, we do not understand tempering, and we do not understand dreaming. We cannot imagine how pleasant it can be in that transcendent, sublime, pure, wonderful realm. If we unfold such a picture before us, it would probably evoke only trembling, not longing. And we confidently believe that we are a great nation, often proudly pointing to our Great Wall as proof, proving that this greatness is locked within the line from Shanhaiguan to Jiayuguan.

Outside the Great Wall is also the desert, and to the west is also the desert. The desert certainly seems completely barren and useless. But that area produces resilient camels, agile steeds, and equally produces brave and solid peoples. The desert has not completely swallowed all life, but instead nurtures a unique vitality. We also call it the "瀚海" (Han Hai, vast sea), and it truly possesses the various qualities of the sea, but also has the hardness and wilderness that the sea lacks. Having floated across the sea and climbed high mountains, without experiencing a desert journey, one cannot be considered to have undergone the most arduous and extraordinary test. We only need to look at history and be amazed that Egyptians, Arabs, Mongols, and Turks all created vast empires. If the theory of the westward migration of the Han people is reliable, then the initial expansion of the Han power may also have benefited from the wild air absorbed from the desert. But once settled, they gradually became peaceful and no longer wanted to endure the tempering of the sand, but only feared and disliked it. Regarding the Xiongnu, the idea that they could come but we could not go was established early on, and the Great Wall is a product of this cowardly idea. Perhaps it once had considerable effect, but this self-made shackle had even greater drawbacks. Some outstanding heroes also tried hard to break the shackles, but soon after being broken, they were repaired again. During the prosperous Tang Dynasty, songs about the hardship of leaving the border satirized ambitious rulers beyond recognition. "Peach blossoms after the horse, snow before the horse, how can one not turn back?" Turn back, turn back, back into the Great Wall, back to Chang'an, back to western Sichuan. By the time the poet expressed the lament, "Liangzhou has fallen for forty years, the Hexi region has been invaded for seven thousand miles. In peaceful times, Anxi was a land of ten thousand miles, but today the border defense is in Fengxiang," it was already too late. After experiencing deeper humiliation, it was realized that "Tang people" was a name to be proud of, and even Genghis Khan's descendants had to be praised sometimes, but few of us understand the desert and want to know the desert. Will the extreme and fierce forever be unrelated to us, and let the desert cultivate the extraordinary spirit of others? The northwest wind is rising; can it bring a little more desert air and arouse our thirst for the desert?

Miscellaneous Notes on Eugene O'Neill's Plays (III) 193

— Yu

Readers, I made a request above, "Let me be like a theater critic, like someone who understands drama." Now, I want to withdraw that request and ask you to consider me as someone who completely doesn't understand drama, and what I write is completely the words of an amateur. The essence of drama is close to poetry. My prejudice (perhaps in many cases, it is actually the case) is that scripts with a strong poetic flavor are only suitable for reading, and to a certain extent, are not very suitable for performance. Shelley once said that poets are the unacknowledged legislators of the world. Although this is not an age of poetry, and poets are despised as worthless by merchants with only fat in their bellies, even now, drama still relies on poetry to evoke people's emotions. Many plays by the Irish poet Yeats are full of such characteristics. Another famous and more progressive Irish playwright, such as Sean O'Casey, also permeates the atmosphere of a poet in "Within the Gates" and "The Moon in the Yellow River." Although the moving parts of a drama's performance are not in those poetic dialogues, and the trend of modern drama also tries to avoid using this technique to enhance the important effects of the performance, the exquisite dialogue and the playwright's fondness for using meaningful soliloquies are still commonly used by great modern playwrights. When we read O'Neill's plays, we can often see that the heroes in his plays rarely stop their actions and make long and profound soliloquies, and although those soliloquies are not in poetic form like those of previous playwrights, we still feel a poetic rhythm in his prose soliloquies. For example, in "The Great God Brown," we can read such passionate dialogues:

"Dion: Brown will still need me—to prove he's living! I've loved, lusted, won love, lost love, sung and cried! I'm a lover of life! I once fulfilled her will. If she deserts me now, it's because I'm too cowardly to turn around and dominate her. I'm not good enough to be her creature; you have to create her, or she'll ask you to destroy yourself."

Dion's following soliloquy even more fully reveals such an atmosphere:

"Dion: (With a painful bewilderment) Why am I afraid to dance? I'm a person who loves musical melodies, elegant singing, and laughter! Why am I afraid to live? I'm a person who loves life, the beauty of the flesh, and the lively face of the earth, sky, and sea! Why am I afraid of love? I'm a person who loves love! Why am I afraid? I'm a person who isn't afraid! Why do I pretend to be arrogant to achieve pity? Why must I hide in inferiority to achieve understanding? Why am I so ashamed of my strength and so enamored with my weakness? Why must I live in a cage, like a criminal, resisting and hating, when I'm a person who loves peace and friendship? (Clasps his hands tightly and raises them up in prayer) Oh God, why was I born without skin, so that I could put on armor for contact or being contacted."

In "Welded," we can also see those poetic sentences jumping between the lines:

"Cape: Listen! Sometimes I wake up late at night—alone in a dark world, in the darkness of billions of years. I feel like I want to shout to God for mercy, because life still exists. Then instinctively I look for you—my hand touches you! You are there—beside me—alive—with you I become a whole person, a true person! Life guides me back through billions of years to reach you. That shows me the unity of the beginning, so that I can believe in the unity of the ending." I don't know if there are any actors who can fully perform the meaning of such powerful sentences and deeply penetrate the hearts of the audience. I think poetic soliloquies are very easy to arouse our emotions when we read them, but when they are performed, I feel that those long and serious soliloquies are the most annoying thing for the audience. I remember before January 28, I went with Ling Mo to the Apollo Cinema on North Sichuan Road (now demolished) to see O'Neill's "Anna Christie." In that play, Greta Garbo played Anna, the late old movie queen Marie Dressler played Marthy, and I have completely forgotten who played the two roles of Chris Christopherson and the young sailor "Burke." The motive for going was admiration, but the result was disappointment. I remember that the dialogue between Marthy and Anna took up a large part. Although Dressler and Garbo were both actors who could act, the directors of the Golden State are almost all vulgar and useless fools, who want to use literary masterpieces to call attention, but do not have the means to express them as vividly as possible. As a result, they become neither fish nor fowl, not only failing to please the original author, but also selling out the audience. Furthermore, the latter part of the script was completely cut off by the director. After Anna and her father rescued Burke from the sea, Anna fell in love with him. Old Christopherson saw this situation and cursed "that old devil sea" on the bow of the ship, and the play closed at this point. As for Anna's inner pain because of Burke's love for her in O'Neill's original play, her frankly telling Burke about her past because she didn't want to deceive him, Burke's angrily leaving after hearing everything, Burke's inability to forget Anna and returning to her place again, and old Christopherson's initial opposition followed by his joy at seeing Burke's return, all these episodes were wiped out by the Hollywood director's sweeping axe. In addition, the sound movie equipment was poor, and my English listening ability was poor, from the opening to the closing, I only understood "dat ole davil, sea (that old devil, sea)." Therefore, I left the cinema with a stomach full of resentment. If, according to some people, going to the movies is not to see the film, but to see the stars, then I should be satisfied since I saw Garbo. However, my purpose was exactly the opposite, so I was disappointed, and I became more convinced of my prejudice: a good script has its own value, not in its performance effect; whether the performance effect is good or bad may not be in itself, but outside of itself. Thus, the value of the performance is one thing, and the value of the drama itself is another thing.

From someone who only has the opportunity to read scripts and not the opportunity to see famous actors perform famous plays, I can only think like this. However, we need to know that the reason why O'Neill is recognized as a great American playwright, and why he is considered a great experimenter in drama, is not only because his drama itself has its special achievements, but also because he has added many new techniques to theatrical acting. For example, the success of the performance of "The Emperor Jones," which we know, is the addition of new techniques, because from the beginning to the close of the play, the audience always hears the sound of drums. The sound of the drums initially beats seventy-two times per minute, according to the normal pulse rate. Later, as the plot becomes more tense, the speed of the drums becomes faster and faster, until finally it sounds like a hair trigger. This sound of the drums naturally achieves the effect of making the audience psychologically tense, and makes the performance of the drama even more tense with this sound. In "Strange Interlude," O'Neill added a new trick - the actor's aside. In this play, in addition to the necessary dialogues, the actors use language to explain their thoughts after each sentence. In other words, the audience can use the actor's aside to understand the true feelings of the characters in the play. In "The Great God Brown," we also see a novel attempt, which is to use masks to represent the dual personality of the characters in the play. When the words spoken by a character express his outer self, that character wears a mask. Conversely, when he is expressing what he wants to say in his heart, he takes off his mask. According to many people, this innovation is not a further step in the technique of modern drama, but a step backward, because we can find such mask performances in Greek drama. In our large country, there are also facial makeup patterns. It cannot be said that there is no similar meaning between masks and facial makeup patterns. However, because O'Neill used masks, the old-fashioned conservatives in our country became dizzy and excited, thinking that they had found a foreign basis in Peking Opera, and that Peking Opera could raise its evaluation, which is nonsense. Speaking of this, I want to make a joke using a few words from Curtis, the protagonist of O'Neill's four-act play "The First Man," who changed from a mining engineer to a geologist, and from a geologist to an anthropologist, and to end this section of the article. Curtis, the protagonist of O'Neill's "The First Man," believes that the earliest home of the human race was in Asia. So he wants to "go to the Himalayas and north-central Asia." In this place, he hopes to find our first ancestor. Although this matter has "only one chance in ten million," he believes that he can at least find "well-founded clues." Perhaps in this place, the first man and his way of life can be found. So Mr. Curtis decided to take his wife to China to complete his great undertaking. But his wife died because of childbirth, and although he decided to come to China, O'Neill's script ended when he was about to set off, so he did not tell us whether he found "the first man" in our large country. However, I don't know if the Peking Man (Sinaihropus Pekinnensis) that we recently discovered is qualified to be called "the first man." However, the Peking Opera that preaches for feudal forces is qualified to be said to be the entertainment of "the first man." This "three-part pot" composed of primitive music, folk miscellaneous genres, and local dramas, has neither the literary value of Yuan Dynasty zaju dramas or Ming Dynasty chuanqi dramas, nor the tight structure of modern European and American dramas, nor the ability to express the meaning of life. Today, there are still many old-fashioned conservatives who believe that many of the already rotten parts in the middle can be reformed. But when I take it apart, I always feel that it no longer has a valuable part, nothing that can be retained. The best way to deal with this drama that expresses the life of "mummies" in museums is to send it to the museum as research material for our archaeologists, or like the antiques in the Ancient Palace Museum, send it to foreign countries to open the eyes of foreigners (but be careful not to lose face abroad). Of course, such words from an amateur like me are very easy to offend when this old treasure of "crown of the crown of thorns, the shoulder of a dead cow" is raising its head again, and when the alchemist's art of refining pills, the Yin-Yang school's apocryphal books, and the Taoist's Tai Chi diagrams have all become symbols of spiritual civilization. But how can a young man care about so much?

My Blessing 194

— Tian Jian

Arise,

Arise,

Don the power

The old Isaiah chapter

Flowing

The people's blood,

Rush to the altar of freedom!

My blessing—

Tears of shame,

Nurturing our seven heads.

Today,

Our seven heads,

Are sharper than the chains around our necks!

Our seven heads,

Are more beautiful than our lives!

Let us,

Cherish our seven heads,

Use our seven heads to strangle the enemy,

Or strangle ourselves...

Commemorating Mr. Lu Xun 195

The Preparatory Committee of the Mr. Lu Xun Commemoration Committee has decided on the following matters to commemorate Mr. Lu Xun: (1) The Committee shall establish a secretariat, divided into two sections: documentation and general affairs; (2) The members of the Committee shall separately contact well-known figures from all walks of life at home and abroad, as well as relatives and friends of Mr. Lu Xun, to prepare for the establishment of a formal commemoration committee; (3) Preliminary procedures for the arrangement of Mr. Lu Xun's burial site shall be discussed and carried out as soon as possible; (4) Designs for a permanent burial site are being solicited; (5) Opinions on commemoration projects are being solicited; (6) Funds for carrying out commemoration projects are being raised; (7) Letters relating to items four and five above should be sent to the Committee's temporary mailing address (c/o Zhou Jianren, Translation Department, Shanghai Commercial Press). Donations should be handed over to the Trust Department of the Bank of China in various locations (Shanghai, Nanjing, Beijing, Tianjin, Qingdao, Hankou, Xi'an, Chengdu, Xiamen, Guangzhou, Japan, Hangzhou, Singapore) for collection, and a receipt from the Committee should be obtained; (8) The Committee's progress and the amount of donations received, as well as the names of donors, will be published in the newspaper from time to time to ensure credibility.

In addition, the Yiwen She (Translation Society), in memory of Mr. Lu Xun, has specially produced a three-color edition postcard of "The Sleeping Mr. Lu Xun," painted by Hiroshi Okumura of Japan. Readers who wish to have this postcard for remembrance, please enclose three cents postage and write to Yiwen She, No. 3 Sunshun Lane, Huanlong Road, Shanghai, and it will be sent to you immediately.

Regarding the "One Pillar House Poetry" Literary Inquisition 196

— Tang Tao

I once borrowed from the edicts of Emperor Qianlong to discuss the causes of the Hu Zhongzao poetry inquisition. The discussion was rudimentary and crude, which was unavoidable. This time, I want to talk about the One Pillar House poetry inquisition. I have not seen the records regarding the Xu Shukui case in the Qing Dynasty's records of literary inquisitions. What I am discussing now is based on several personal notebooks. My personal observations are limited, and short essays are not suitable for extensive citations, so shallowness and crudeness are, of course, still unavoidable.

Xu Shukui was from Dongtai County, Jiangsu Province. He was a successful candidate in the imperial examination during the Wu-Wu year of Qianlong. He authored "One Pillar House Poetry" in six volumes, "One Pillar House Small Topic Poetry" in one volume, "Poetry of Tao" in one volume, "Lectures on the Doctrine of the Mean" in one volume, "Miscellaneous Writings from the Qu Juantang Studio" in one volume, "Thoughts for Future Generations" in two volumes, and "Abstracts of the Analects" in two volumes. The first four types were published by his son, Xu Huaizu, in the twenty-eighth year of Qianlong, while the last three were copied and stored at home. After the case came to light in the forty-third year, they were submitted and destroyed. During his lifetime, he seemed to have greatly admired Lü Wanlin and was deeply influenced by him. This is evidenced by the memorials of the ministers who later tried the case, saying: "Xu Shukui, who is already dead in this case, was a successful candidate in the imperial examination of this dynasty. He dared to take the already destroyed heresies of the rebel Lü Liuliang as his guiding principle in compiling poetry collections and lectures, which is already rebellious and unlawful," which can be confirmed. It is evident that there were some intense anti-Manchu sentiments within. Speaking objectively, among the many literary inquisitions of the Qing Dynasty, the One Pillar House poetry inquisition can be considered a case with more factual basis and not entirely unjust.

As for the cause of this case, according to the same memorial—the memorial from Grand Scholar Agui and others regarding the joint trial of the Xu Shukui case:

Xu Huaizu had previously purchased several acres of land from Cai Jiashu's younger brother, Cai Yun, in the same county, for a contract price of one thousand four hundred taels of silver. After his death, Cai Jiashu, because there were ancestral graves within the land, wanted to redeem it in the winter of the forty-second year for nine hundred and sixty taels of silver, but Xu Shitian, Xu Huaizu's son, refused. Consequently, he filed a lawsuit. At that time, the county had already posted a notice in accordance with the imperial decree to investigate and collect prohibited books. In the early fourth month of this year, Xu Shitian heard that Cai Jiashu, because he did not agree to redeem the land, wanted to accuse him of prohibited content in the books printed by his ancestor. On the sixth day of the month, the criminal submitted all the printed books and printing plates to the county. The county magistrate, Tu Yaolong, immediately approved them for review. On the eighth day, Cai Jiashu also submitted books such as "One Pillar House Poetry" and the biography of Xu Shukui written by Shen Deqian to the county, claiming in his submission: fearing there may be prohibited content, without extracting the phrases. The county magistrate, seeing that the biography stated that his "One Pillar House Poetry Collection" had already been printed and listed the titles of Xu Shukui's unpublished books, and that the books submitted by Xu Shitian were not complete, issued a warrant on the eleventh day, specifically ordering the submission of the missing books. Xu Shitian then submitted the three unpublished manuscripts to the county on the sixteenth day, which were approved for submission to the Bureau...

The land disputes of wealthy gentry often became the fuse for the destruction of property and beheadings. This time, it was the same. The county magistrate ordered Xu Shitian to allocate ten acres of grave land to Cai Jiashu, which could have been the end of it, but Cai Jiashu suddenly began to investigate the dates on which Xu Shitian submitted the books. What he found out happened to be not the first date—the sixth—but the date of the supplemental submission—the sixteenth. Thus, he believed that Xu Shitian's submission was after his accusation, and that the county magistrate was bribed and changed the accusation to a submission, intentionally trying to exonerate Xu Shitian. He extracted the rebellious sentences from "One Pillar House Poetry" and reported them to the Jiangning Provincial Administration Office. Lu Yan, a secretary of the Provincial Administration Office, wrote on the back of the petition: "What does this have to do with you! Order the Yangzhou Prefecture Xie Qikun to investigate and try the case, and wrote in the official document: "Searching for books was originally for those with rebellious writings and satirical poems from the late Ming and early Qing dynasties. Those that are indeed prohibited should be collected and submitted. As for those who discuss scriptures and write articles, expressing them in poems and verses, if only the wording is careless and involves suspicion, and there is no evidence of actual rebellion, the person who first accuses should be punished according to the law with the crime they falsely accused. This is clearly stated in the regulations. If Cai Jiashu is using this to falsely accuse out of resentment, intending to harm him, interrogate him strictly and deliberate." At this time, "One Pillar House Poetry Collection" and other books had already been submitted by Dongtai County to the Jiangning Book Bureau, but because they were not affixed with labels, they were returned to the county for labeling. The process of Lu Yan's remarks and the inside story of the Jiangning Book Bureau can be seen in the testimony of Tao Yi, the Jiangning Provincial Administration Commissioner, who stated:

The Jiangsu Province handled the matter of collecting surviving books, originally setting up a general bureau in Jiangning, dispatching clerks with some knowledge of literature to manage affairs in the bureau. When books were submitted by various prefectures and counties, they were compiled into thousands of volumes and sent to my office, and then submitted to the governor-general for review. As for the books submitted by Xu Shitian of Dongtai County, because I was in the midst of praying for rain during a major drought, I did not actually look at the county report and "One Pillar House Poetry" and other books. The bureau clerk drafted a reply to the county to add labels. In the middle of June, Cai Jiashu came to my office to accuse him, and my secretary Lu Yan ordered the Yangzhou Prefecture to investigate. And in the urging of drafts by the book clerk, he added the words of "false accusation and punishment," which I did not examine carefully, and I signed and approved it. After the education commissioner Liu Yong impeached me, I returned from Xuzhou after inspecting the disaster, and examined the documents, and said to my secretary: "The documents were handled very wrongly." He said: "I had received an imperial decree that self-reporting would be exempt from punishment. This book was submitted by Xu Shitian first, and Cai Jiashu came to accuse him again, so this approval is not a big mistake." I said: "It is still a mistake."...

Xu Shitian submitted the books first, and Dongtai County did not change the accusation to self-reporting. This is a fact; submitting the books first did not allow him to receive the imperial grace of "self-reporting exempts from punishment," which is also a fact. Because the provisions of the Qing Dynasty for submitting prohibited books stated that self-reporting could exempt from punishment, but self-reporting knowing that someone wanted to accuse was only allowed to reduce punishment, but not exempt from punishment. Xu Shitian's self-reporting was only three days away from Cai Jiashu's accusation. The water was already muddy, and it could not wash away the sins of the ancestors. Lu Yan's so-called "received an imperial decree, self-reporting exempts from punishment" was not applicable here; however, his order to the Yangzhou Prefecture to investigate strictly, "If it is a false accusation, punish them according to the law with the crime they falsely accused," in my opinion, was still a fair argument. However, this fair argument, when placed on Emperor Qianlong's scale, would inevitably appear biased, which was also inevitable.

In addition to citing Lü Liuliang's "heresies," the most rebellious thing in the books of One Pillar House was considered to be his own poems with the two lines "In the Ming Dynasty, expect to spread my wings, and take off to Qingdu in one fell swoop." Other accusations included: "Do not let it flow into the soil, for it has long been mixed with filth"; "Which sleeping dragon will follow"; "Dare to say that Heaven is not intentional in resuming brightness"; "Although the court is in chaos, the mountains and forests are well governed"; "Jiangbei has long been without clean soil"; "The sideburns are not the current style, no need to praise my unique purity"; "I do not know the day when the road is cleared of dust, can there be circumstances that offend the imperial chariot"; "Where in the world can be home"; "The old heavenly heart was originally a dream, and recently the world affairs are becoming increasingly absurd" and other sentences. Among these, some are obvious, some are obscure, some are factual, and some are fabricated, and cannot be generalized. But the most interesting thing is the names of Xu Shoufa and Shen Chengzhuo, who are listed as proofreaders. According to the meaning of those old judges, they thought that the "meaning of the two people's names are not clear, and the four characters together are an implied satire of the hair-shaving system of this dynasty," so it is equivalent to "planting a party and howling, which is especially hateful." Now let's look at the testimonies of these two people:

Xu Shoufa testified: Because he had long hair when he was first born, his childhood name was Fa Er. At the age of nine, he followed Xu Shukui to study, who named him Shoufa, and told him the meaning of "body, hair, and skin, received from parents," so the character was Shouzhi. He had heard Xu Shukui say: Shaving the head in this dynasty is not as good-looking as not shaving in the Ming Dynasty. And he claimed that his learning was like that in the Ming Dynasty, which could be compared with Tang Jingzhou's Dong Sibai, but today's people can't see it. He only studied with him for two years, and later he changed to another teacher, changed his name to Shoufa, and took the examination to enter school, which can be checked. According to Shen Chengzhuo's testimony: He studied with Xu Shukui when he was young, who named him. He also heard Xu Shukui say that the Ming Dynasty had hair, and now he shaved his head, which means Zhzhuo. According to each testimony: The names of the proofreaders were added by his son Xu Huaizu when he printed the book after Xu Shukui's death.

In the testimony, some of the words are clearly the result of being implicated, which can be understood at a glance. This is similar to the current criminals who claim that the confessions in the police station are forced by the environment and have to confess, which has the same hidden pain. Even if Xu Shukui named them intentionally, it can't be considered a satire, let alone a rebellion. But what was the result? Not only did Shoufa Chengzhuo, but even their heads were moved together.

Neither keeping their hair nor their heads, this is how to set a good example for those who caused the country to fall.

The ending of the One Pillar House poetry inquisition was that Xu Shukui and his son Xu Huaizu were already dead and were to be dismembered and their heads displayed in accordance with the law of lingchi (death by a thousand cuts); the grandsons of the main offender, Xu Shichu and Xu Shishu, were to be beheaded immediately, and their relatives were implicated; the proofreaders Xu Shoufa, Shen Chengzhuo, the provincial administrator Tao Yi, and his secretary Lu Yan were to be beheaded immediately; Mao Cheng, whose alias was Huang Bin, who wrote the postscript for the poetry of Tao, was to receive one hundred strokes of the cane and exiled three thousand miles; the prefect Xie Qikun was to be sent to the military station for service; the county magistrate Tu Yaolong was to receive one hundred strokes of the cane and one year of imprisonment; Shen Deqian was removed from the local worthies shrine, his official title and posthumous title were revoked, and the imperial stele bestowed upon him was destroyed. Later, except for all the "beheaded immediately" being changed to "beheaded in custody, executed after the autumn," there were no other changes. As for the plaintiff Cai Jiashu, he gained nothing; if it weren't for the "Emperor's" special grace, he would have almost been accused of "knowing the truth early on, not reporting it immediately, and accusing him out of resentment due to land disputes."

The wealthy gentry often have a dream, thinking that by using the hands of others to overwhelm the opponent, they can stand out. Therefore, they are good at being slaves and good at being traitors. However, the blood shed due to the Qing Dynasty's literary inquisitions ultimately soaked the ambition of the patriots and submerged the Manchu's world. The wind of accusation has not stopped, and the blood of the patriots has not dried. This is worthy of deep thought.

Shen Bao Local Supplement

Talks

What Crime is There in "Hair" and "Powder"? 197

— Wu Ci

It seems that women cannot live without rouge and powder is a universally recognized truth. But no matter how much women love it themselves, a certain force in society does not approve of them dressing up in a showy manner. Saying they don't love it, the faces of their own wives are "娇滴滴越显红自 [radiant and fair]."

Not long ago, there was news in this city of prohibiting female teachers from perming their hair and applying rouge, on the grounds that the huge import figures of the customs were all caused by them, and they were also "not worthy of being teachers"! Recently, Turkey also has similar measures, and said more grandly: "If female teachers imitate actresses and wear brightly colored clothes and fashionable makeup, they will inevitably have a significant adverse impact on the students, so it should be strictly prohibited..."

Summarizing these views, they are mostly against female teachers applying rouge and powder, while other wealthy people's wives or concubines are not subject to the prohibition. Even if they sometimes affect the huge import figures of the customs, or can also "have an adverse impact on society," that is another matter.

The above is considered past facts. And mostly initiated by men. Now it's the women's turn to speak. On the broadcast propaganda of the Suiyuan Bandit Suppression Comfort Committee for Women, a certain lady exclaimed loudly:

"Especially for our women's world, if we can buy one less box of face powder or perm our hair one less time, wouldn't the soldiers at the front have one more vest or one more pair of gloves?"

The meaning is very clear, to make the soldiers at the front have one more vest or one more pair of gloves, just buy one less box of face powder or perm the hair one less time will do. This is the opinion of the women themselves, and this opinion is also worth cherishing. The motivation to comfort the soldiers at the front and being related to social morality are completely different. In such an environment, we can boldly oppose maintaining social morality, but there is no reason to oppose comforting the front-line soldiers. For the sake of comforting, buying one less box of face powder or perming the hair one less time are all extremely appropriate.

But there is still a question here, that is "buy less" or "perm less". The so-called "less" is only reducing the quantity of "buying" and "perming", but not absolutely not "buying" or "perming". Wouldn't it be more beneficial to be able to absolutely not "buy" or not "perm"? However, the women's own opinion is like this.

Such an opinion is very innocent. It shows that women have not given up on cosmetics under the premise of comforting the bandit suppression soldiers. If I am not oversensitive, I would say that the lady who said these words had put on powder and permed her hair before going to the radio station. Why are the opinions of women themselves so opposed to the opinions of some people in society? This is a mystery.

Many people are confused by this mystery. They do not understand why there is such a distance between the two. In fact, this is just a difference in the perspective of interests. Some people must have women dress very simply "in public" in order to show their dignity, while women themselves feel that washing off their makeup will degrade their position. With such a contradiction, although prohibitions are prohibited, applying rouge and powder is still applying rouge and powder, and even under extremely important preconditions, they are not willing to give up this opinion.

If you put your own interests behind, the result will be different. If you don't believe me, there is a report as evidence:

"Ms. Li Shuqing from Shanxi Province, thinking of the country's crisis, petitioned to destroy her family to relieve the national crisis, and presented the document to Chairman Zhao, saying...I have 170 acres of land and a shop in Jingle County...Except for half of the shop that will be reserved for my husband's recuperation, the rest will be offered to the government...I can make a living by serving the country and society or as a home tutor..."

If we have Ms. Li's spirit, not to be shaken by the environment, Ms. Li did not say not to apply rouge or perm her hair, but her contribution is much greater than not applying rouge or perming her hair. Without striving for universal thrift, without striving to cultivate the spirit of sacrificing the individual, and calculating every detail about applying rouge and powder, it is really "abandoning the fundamental and pursuing the superficial, I do not know what can be achieved!"

Taiping Bridge 198

— Sheng Lu

The sound of the night watchman's clapper was not far away when a group of people with horse lanterns walked towards Taiping Bridge. The four leading men were dressed as servants, holding willow branches picked from the roadside, with the bark rolled back and tied into a ball of flowers hanging at the top. As they walked, the willow ball swung back and forth in the dim light like their victorious hearts.

Page 18

When they reached the bottom of Taiping Bridge, the four men gave a coded command. The men behind them in short clothes immediately rolled up their sleeves, tugged up their trouser legs, and the crowbars, ropes, iron pry bars, and all the tools were distributed in everyone's hands. It turned out that they were starting to dismantle Taiping Bridge.

Since the rickshaws were popular in the county town, the high stone bridges were either dismantled or filled in. Only the few large bridges passing over the official river cost a little project to rebuild the flat stone bridges. Taiping Bridge was located in the westernmost part of the county town, close to the border of the neighboring county. The nearby areas were all poor households and temporary accommodations for people from other provinces, so Taiping Bridge would not hinder the traffic of rickshaws, because the people living near Taiping Bridge simply did not have the money to take rickshaws, and they had strong legs like the rickshaw pullers. Moreover, Taiping Bridge had been passed down in this county town as the authority capable of dominating the residents' peace: no matter whether the rich or the prominent married, the bride and groom's sedan chairs had to circle around Taiping Bridge to ensure the peace of the whole house. Therefore, after rickshaws had been popular for a year, all the bridge piers had been demolished into flat ground, but no one had proposed to demolish Taiping Bridge.

But these buildings, bridges, and stone steps built thousands of years ago are all excellent materials, and the contractors who dismantled the bridges made a fortune for each bridge they dismantled. Then, where there is profit, some people dare to come out and propose to dismantle the only remaining bridge, Taiping Bridge, in the county town, because the contractors have local gentlemen backing them. It was during some meeting that the proposal to demolish Taiping Bridge was passed for the sake of traffic convenience.

The news and the execution were equally fast. On the one hand, the internally appointed contractor obtained the bidding rights for the demolition of the bridge, and on the other hand, the residents near Taiping Bridge gathered together to deal with the demolition contractor...

One morning, a man carrying a wooden sign and beating a gong walked west along the street to report that Taiping Bridge was going to be demolished. This is a custom. It is said that when demolishing a bridge or breaking ground, children cannot run out of the house, otherwise their souls will be taken away to suppress the project. But the children's souls were not taken away, but the gong beater was injured at the foot of Taiping Bridge and died in less than a day.

This is naturally an expression of the anger of the residents who believe in Taiping Bridge's ability to dominate the peace of the town. So the contractor consulted the great gentlemen behind him, and the gentlemen naturally held a so-called meeting. —Originally, it was easy for the great gentlemen to deal with the small people, but in this matter, it was feared that someone would be killed. If someone was killed, it would naturally alarm the county government. Then, this big fortune that was steadily in hand might be overturned by those foolish little people.

The project of demolishing Taiping Bridge was cold for a month.

This day, they started work in the middle of the night, which was the result of a month of deliberation. When the people who got up the earliest in the city got up, the bridge piers of Taiping Bridge had already been dismantled. The four servants were divided into two groups, protecting the workers like guards. Two people in a group were sweating and laboriously dismantling the stone steps with iron pry bars, which looked several times more laborious than paving a stone bridge.

After hiding their children at home, the small people gradually gathered around the bridge abutment. Several strong men stood up in front and questioned the servants, asking them why they were demolishing Taiping Bridge. The servants who came out to do the work had forgotten their own status. They gently swung the willow balls in their hands and used their eyebrows to give the spokesmen the most embarrassing answer.

In addition to having strong legs like rickshaws, these strong men also had iron-like fists. Seeing the dog-like fangs and claws, they would deal with them in the same way as they had dealt with the gong beater.

They immediately retreated two steps, pointed to the forehead of the man at the front with the willow ball, and cursed:

"Good! You pigs, who beat up Ah Gen, arrest him and send him to the yamen to face trial!"

What small person is not afraid of facing trial? The women rushed forward and grabbed the men's arms. It would be better to demolish Taiping Bridge than to face trial. Even if the poor people could ensure the peace of the whole house, they still had the problem of eating, which was more important than peace. Men are their best meal ticket!

On the day of completion, when several gentlemen came to inspect, the bright rickshaws were easily dragged across the bridge.

Eagle's Cry 199

— Chen Wu

— Village Notes —

When the clear, trembling cry of an eagle circled in the sky, I often unconsciously walked to the center of the courtyard, looked up at the sky, and searched for the eagle that sang the greatness of nature and the embrace of nature.

The eagle's song reminded me of a strong man, of a great man who soared high in the sky and looked down on the world, of a free, unrestrained, and upright man. It is the majestic, inspiring, and full-of-spirit cry of one who faces everything squarely.

Often I searched the sky, far away, or right above my head, and I could see the eagle's entire body. My heart was broadened, and my heart seemed to be on the eagle's back. I was also circling, looking down on the sky. For half a day, until I saw it fly far away, I disappointedly returned to the dark room.

How good it would be to be able to fly? Although many people have flown past with human intelligence, I still only see a few feet around, as if watching the world in a prison.

I am grateful to those few eagles. When the sun climbs above the horizon, I hear the clear, sharp song. Sometimes when the sun falls behind that high mountain, they seem to be chasing the sun, flying west, circling, and I forget that the surrounding mountains have trapped my eyes and that I have forgotten the silent village life that is almost half-dead. There is young blood jumping in my brain, and my thoughts escape the shabby calculations of life.

One day, a neighbor's child told me that a family had caught two eagles. When I heard this, it was as if my own brother had been arrested. I rushed to the house and saw a young eagle locked in a bamboo cage. On the ground, there was another large eagle lying softly, bleeding from its abdomen, its eyes wide open, and its sharp beak hooked on the edge of a broken table. I saw the smiles on the faces of the few people in the family, with their yellow teeth, and in my heart I cursed them: despicable people, it does you no harm, but you use what means to assassinate it and capture them?

I looked at the eagle in the cage. It was jumping, its green eyes staring hatefully at people, shaking its hemp-like feathers, and suddenly raised its head and cried out clearly. Startled by this unexpected cry, I was determined to listen to the brave man who seemed to appear before my eyes. He was questioning the unnecessary harm to him and the shameful actions against him.

I heard the eagle cry again. After this, I heard the eagle cry and felt that there was an angry voice cursing humanity in their cries.

Feelings After Buying Books 200

— Fang Jie

I've always had a penchant for buying books; however, due to economic constraints, I've never been able to indulge in a truly satisfying book-buying spree. Consequently, to this day, my book collection remains quite small.

What I found most regrettable was that last October, we had moved to a county town in Zhejiang. At that time, I was holding down a minor job, earning several tens of dollars a month, which was comparatively a period of economic ease for me; therefore, I desperately wanted to buy more books. Every day, I eagerly searched for bookstores on the street, but in reality, I unexpectedly encountered another setback: I couldn't find a single decent bookstore in that place. By the time I returned at the end of the year, my pockets were once again completely empty.

Today, I finally managed to buy a few books, but it evoked memories. I feel there are two issues regarding buying books that need to be discussed:

1. The price of books is too high;

2. The locality of books is too narrow.

Due to the first limitation, books are out of reach for readers with low purchasing power; due to the second limitation, books are out of reach for readers confined to remote areas.

This cannot but be seen as a huge crisis in the reading world! Booksellers, focused on shiny silver coins, may disregard it, but this weakens the dissemination of books and reduces their audience.

Books are a record of culture. Now, as our nation is encountering an unprecedentedly difficult period of struggle, culture is precisely what is needed to exert its highest degree of function to demonstrate its great power, so that it can vigorously fulfill the task of national defense. The progress of culture is not only a vertical deepening, but also a horizontal expansion; it is not only an upper-level study, but also a lower-level activity. In order to broadly appeal, then the readership with low purchasing power and the readership confined to remote areas are precisely the best targets! The most important targets!

In fact, these two types of readers are precisely the bearers of the times, and guaranteeing victory must be achieved through them. So, should we kick these heroic partners aside?

I understand their pain, and have personally experienced it: hovering at the entrance of bookstores, harboring a strong desire, craning one's neck to look at the book list. Occasionally seeing a "masterpiece," the heart feels pricked, knowing it is an inaccessible treasure, but still eagerly flipping through it with affection, even holding it in one's hands for a long time, cherishing it. Finally, a stick comes crashing down: the price of the book looms like an enlarged wheel turning in front of you, and one can only bid it farewell with a red face, watching it fall into someone else's arms. At this time, one not only feels pain and resentment, but even jealousy and anger towards others. Can the world allow such cruel facts to exist?

As for readers confined to remote areas, they are also pitiful. The appearance of new culture is almost like a moon in the sea, forever just a shadow, and the two seem to have forgotten each other. Last year in Zhejiang, I lived in a relatively large county town, and transportation was also convenient; but in the only institution of higher learning there—just a middle school—I saw a heartbreaking phenomenon. A school publication was supposed to introduce the latest literary books, but the list consisted entirely of early works from the Creation Society; and for them, it was considered unprecedented and novel!

Those engaged in cultural work, please pay attention to these!

Opening "Water Margin: Tiger Fighting"

Heroes have always been strong-willed. They fear no tigers and leopards on high peaks. Not believing the innkeeper's roundabout words. Seeing the notice, posted far and wide. On Jingyang Ridge, there is a striped tiger nearby. Lonely travelers risk their lives. Beating gongs to alert the crowd to be wary. Wu Song laughs and declares. Tipsy, he takes his stick up the mountain. Coincidentally, the fifteenth night is bright. From afar, the steep ridge is densely forested. He doesn't believe that there is a tiger's den in the hollow. Opening "Autumn Window Lament" (Mimi Collection Launch Issue)

A bolt from the blue is thought-provoking. Why create grievances and secretly hurt people? I want to comfort my parents and make them happy. Be content, frugal, and thrifty. Last night, a sudden letter arrived. Therefore, I hurriedly bid farewell and turned home. Hearing my mother's words, my heart is breaking. Although it's not true, it's hard to argue. I've never been to brothels or flower lanes. Let alone female entertainers. I am a respectable descendant of the Fengxi family. How could I lose my integrity and learn to be modern? My family is poor and I haven't studied much. At the young age of thirteen, I learned a trade. I am grateful for the recommendations of the elders. I am a radio broadcaster at a middle school. A year has passed in the blink of an eye. It neither benefits nor harms the company. I only hope that I will flourish and progress day by day. So that one day, I can establish a career and make a name for myself. Who knew that trouble would arise out of nowhere. Causing me to feel ashamed before my clan. What are you saying? No wind rises. No waves surge. What are you saying? No shrimp jumps. The water doesn't move. I am a wronged soul this life. I want to throw myself into the Pujiang River and become a water spirit. But I haven't repaid my parents' nurturing grace.

Jiang Binchu "Three Smiles"

New Voice (1380) 7:00 PM to 8:00 PM

Opening "Xunyang Lute"

Tonight in Xunyang, an autumn on the river. The moonlight clearly sends off a departing boat. The old woman married to a merchant. The lute laments forever new sorrows. My heart is breaking, the guest lingers, tears fall like drops on the Jiujiang River. The mournful strings fly into my ears. I already see cold flowers hiding willows in shame. Blue silk accompanies a lone boat, the wind is tight, except for the sound of bitter bamboo islands. Touching the hands, the pearl concerns is lost. Floating life, old dreams of brocade entangling the head. Reeds and lonely moorings surrounded by land. The pattern on her skirt is of old pomegranates. Leftover Sounds

The Yingyin Amateur Society is now offering thanks to listeners who have always supported our society. We have obtained consent from Zhen De Pharmaceutical Factory to widely distribute their interesting Kangfu Ten Clever Boards. However, due to excessive costs, starting this Sunday, we will hold weekly riddle-guessing prize games to increase interest. The restriction method is that during the broadcast, only the names of the songs will be announced, and listeners must guess the names of the singers who performed them. Those who guess more than half correctly will be awarded a Kangfu Ten Clever Board. The method is simple and quite interesting. Please pay attention to our society's broadcast times and announcements at any time. For inquiries, please send a letter to Mr. Chen Tao, 41, Lane 155, South Chengdu Road, addressed to the society.

The Realist Opera Society has printed and distributed a "Love's Lingering" song selection to all sectors. Those who wish to obtain a copy should include return postage and send it to the society c/o Huatai Radio Station, No. 10 Xiaoben Lane, Gongping Road, or Tianhua Pharmacy at the intersection of Huade Road and Wuchang Road.

Movies

Chaplin's New Film: Regency Period 206

— Hua

Chaplin has four new scripts following "Modern Times." One is a highly satirical novel published in New York's Modern Review, set in Hollywood, depicting the life of a big producer. Chaplin himself is scheduled to play the main character, the big producer. He discovers a young woman among the extras, tries to marry her, but later the young woman runs off with most of his property. It is also scheduled that the young woman will be played by Paulette Goddard.

But later he planned another work, written by a young writer, with Chaplin and Goddard as the main characters. The general idea is that Chaplin is living happily, a millionaire with a very beautiful and intelligent wife. Suddenly, the Second World War breaks out, and he is drafted into the army. On the battlefield, he is a soldier subordinate to a former servant in his house. He is regarded as a spy, and even performs outstanding service. When he returns home after the war, his wife has left and his family is broken, and he becomes a pitiful, lonely wanderer. At this time, he falls in love with Paulette Goddard—a woman who has suffered the same misfortune, and he eventually dies bitterly.

The third is a script specifically exposing the ugliness of Hollywood. He said he wants to declare: "The true face of Hollywood, which so many people in the world yearn for," and he uses a profound pen to dissect the capitalistic atmosphere and decadent life of Hollywood.

In addition, he has many new film plans. Among these new films, after much selection and consideration, he finally officially decided on two new films. One of them, which has already begun filming, is called Regency. We will temporarily translate it as "Regency Period." The original work of this script is by the modern British novelist D.L. Murry, the screenwriter is R.V. Bdley, the director is Chaplin, and the main actress is Paulette Goddard. It is said that he does not plan to appear in this film. One thing that deserves our attention is that this film is entirely spoken with dialogue, and the dialogue in the play is written by the great American playwright Louis Sinclair. Its value is imaginable.

The second film will only be starred by him and her, and will be started after "Regency Period" is finished. The content and naming of the film have not yet been announced, but it is only known that Chaplin in this film will appear to us in a "modernized" appearance. He will abandon his small hat, torn shoes, and tattered clothes and put on modern fashionable clothes. It is said that this is his attempt to "change his style," but the "no-speaking doctrine" is still something he holds onto tightly.

Yuan Meiyun in "Guangling Tide" 207

Star Intelligence 208

Since the release of "United Hearts," business has been excellent. Following this film, Ying Yunwei will engage in Yang Hansheng's new work "Night Flight," a work with smuggling issues and student movements as its theme.

Chen Boer has not yet decided on a new work after "United Hearts." Yuan Muzhi is preparing his new work "Crossroads," which he wrote and directed.

"Crossroads" has been filmed for two-thirds, and there are still some very important exterior shots to be taken.

"Night Flight" must also be completed before the end of the lunar year, and filming will begin after "Crossroads" completes its exterior shots. In this way, the two factories will have three films filming at the same time.

Many students visit the filming of "Crossroads," including those from Jiaotong University, Daxia University, Guanghua University, and Fudan University, perhaps because this film depicts the lives of unemployed college students.
